<ul><li><a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a></li></ul>

<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a></li></ul>

</div>
<div class="plainlist">
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a></li></ul>

<p>The <b>Gupta Empire</b> was an <a href="/wiki/Outline_of_ancient_India" title="Outline of ancient India">ancient Indian</a> empire which existed from the early 4th century CE to late 6th century CE. At its zenith, from approximately 319 to 467 CE, it covered much of the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_Dynasty_–_MSN_Encarta_4-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta_Dynasty_–_MSN_Encarta-4">[4]</a></sup> This period is considered as the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Age_of_India" title="Golden Age of India">Golden Age of India</a> by historians.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[note 1]</a></sup> The ruling dynasty of the empire was founded by the king Sri <a href="/wiki/Gupta_(king)" title="Gupta (king)">Gupta</a>; the most notable rulers of the dynasty were <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_I" title="Chandragupta I">Chandragupta I</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a>. The 5th-century CE <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a> credits the Guptas with having conquered about twenty-one kingdoms, both in and outside India, including the kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Parasikas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Huna_people" title="Huna people">Hunas</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a>, tribes located in the west and east <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus_river" title="Oxus river">Oxus valleys</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Kinnara_Kingdom" title="Kinnara Kingdom">Kinnaras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiratas" title="Kiratas">Kiratas</a>, and others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raghu_Vamsa_v_4.60–75_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raghu_Vamsa_v_4.60–75-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112–18_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112–18-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The high points of this period are the great cultural developments which took place primarily during the reigns of Samudragupta, Chandragupta II and Kumaragupta I. Many Hindu epics and literary sources, such as <a href="/wiki/Mahabharata" title="Mahabharata">Mahabharata</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ramayana" title="Ramayana">Ramayana</a>, were canonised during this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> The Gupta period produced scholars such as <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varahamihira" title="Varahamihira">Varahamihira</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vatsyayana" title="Vatsyayana">Vatsyayana</a> who made great advancements in many academic fields.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVidya_Dhar_Mahajan1990540_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVidya_Dhar_Mahajan1990540-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keay_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keay-14">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/History_of_science_and_technology_in_the_Indian_subcontinent" title="History of science and technology in the Indian subcontinent">Science</a> and political administration reached new heights during the Gupta era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keay_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Keay-14">[13]</a></sup> The period gave rise to achievements in architecture, sculpture, and painting that "set standards of form and taste [that] determined the whole subsequent course of art, not only in India but far beyond her borders".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199487_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199487-16">[15]</a></sup> Strong trade ties also made the region an important cultural centre and established the region as a base that would influence nearby kingdoms and regions in India and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (March 2016)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup> The <a href="/wiki/Puranas" title="Puranas">Puranas</a>, earlier long poems on a variety of subjects, are also thought to have been committed to written texts around this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199487_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199487-16">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dikshitar_1993_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dikshitar_1993-18">[17]</a></sup> Hinduism was followed by the rulers and the Brahmins flourished in the Gupta empire but the Guptas tolerated people of other faiths as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[18]</a></sup>
</p><p>The empire eventually died out because of factors such as substantial loss of territory and imperial authority caused by their own erstwhile feudatories, as well as the invasion by the <a href="/wiki/Huna_people" title="Huna people">Huna peoples</a> (<a href="/wiki/Kidarites" title="Kidarites">Kidarites</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alchon_Huns" title="Alchon Huns">Alchon Huns</a>) from <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989264–69_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989264–69-20">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rene_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rene-21">[20]</a></sup> After the collapse of the Gupta Empire in the 6th century, India was again ruled by numerous regional kingdoms.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Origin">Origin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Origin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

</tr><tr><td class="sidebar-navbar"><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1063604349">.mw-parser-output .navbar{display:inline;font-size:88%;font-weight:normal}.mw-parser-output .navbar-collapse{float:left;text-align:left}.mw-parser-output .navbar-boxtext{word-spacing:0}.mw-parser-output .navbar ul{display:inline-block;white-space:nowrap;line-height:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::before{margin-right:-0.125em;content:"[ "}.mw-parser-output .navbar-brackets::after{margin-left:-0.125em;content:" ]"}.mw-parser-output .navbar li{word-spacing:-0.125em}.mw-parser-output .navbar a>span,.mw-parser-output .navbar a>abbr{text-decoration:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-mini abbr{font-variant:small-caps;border-bottom:none;text-decoration:none;cursor:inherit}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-full{font-size:114%;margin:0 7em}.mw-parser-output .navbar-ct-mini{font-size:114%;margin:0 4em}</style><div class="navbar plainlinks hlist navbar-mini"><ul><li class="nv-view"><a href="/wiki/Template:Gupta_Empire" title="Template:Gupta Empire"><abbr title="View this template">v</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-talk"><a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Gupta_Empire" title="Template talk:Gupta Empire"><abbr title="Discuss this template">t</abbr></a></li><li class="nv-edit"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit"><abbr title="Edit this template">e</abbr></a></li></ul></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>The homeland of the Guptas is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198979_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198979-22">[21]</a></sup> According to one theory, they originated in the present-day lower-Doab region of <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[22]</a></sup> where most of the inscriptions and coin hoards of the early Gupta kings have been discovered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly198714_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly198714-24">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198939_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198939-25">[24]</a></sup> This theory is also supported by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purana" title="Purana">Purana</a>, as argued by the proponents, that mention the territory of the early Gupta kings as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Prayaga" title="Prayaga">Prayaga</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saketa" title="Saketa">Saketa</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Magadha" title="Magadha">Magadha</a> areas in the <a href="/wiki/Ganges" title="Ganges">Ganges</a> basin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly19872_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly19872-26">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal19892_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal19892-27">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another prominent theory locates the Gupta homeland in the present-day <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region in Ganges basin, based on the account of the 7th-century Chinese Buddhist monk <a href="/wiki/Yijing_(monk)" title="Yijing (monk)">Yijing</a>. According to Yijing, king Che-li-ki-to (identified with the dynasty's founder <i>Shri</i> <a href="/wiki/Gupta_(king)" title="Gupta (king)">Gupta</a>) built a temple for Chinese pilgrims near Mi-li-kia-si-kia-po-no (apparently a transcription of <a href="/wiki/M%E1%B9%9Bga%C5%9Bikh%C4%81vana" title="Mṛgaśikhāvana">Mriga-shikha-vana</a>). Yijing states that this temple was located more than 40 <i><a href="/wiki/Yojana" title="Yojana">yojanas</a></i> east of <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a>, which would mean it was situated somewhere in the modern Bengal region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly19877–11_28-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly19877–11-28">[27]</a></sup> Another proposal is that the early Gupta kingdom extended from Prayaga in the west to northern Bengal in the east.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly198712_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDilip_Kumar_Ganguly198712-29">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Gupta records do not mention the dynasty's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Varna_in_Hinduism" title="Varna in Hinduism">varna</a> (social class).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198944_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198944-30">[29]</a></sup> Some historians, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/A.S._Altekar" title="A.S. Altekar">A.S. Altekar</a>, have theorised that they were of <a href="/wiki/Vaishya" title="Vaishya">Vaishya</a> origin, as certain ancient Indian texts prescribe the name "Gupta" for the members of the Vaishya varna.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198982_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198982-31">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198942_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198942-32">[31]</a></sup> According to historian <a href="/wiki/Ram_Sharan_Sharma" title="Ram Sharan Sharma">R. S. Sharma</a>, the Vaishyas – who were traditionally associated with trade – may have become rulers after resisting oppressive taxation by the previous rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[32]</a></sup> Critics of the Vaishya-origin theory point out that the suffix Gupta features in the names of several non-Vaishyas before as well as during the Gupta period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar19814_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar19814-34">[33]</a></sup> and the dynastic name "Gupta" may have simply derived from the name of the family's first king <a href="/wiki/Gupta_(king)" title="Gupta (king)">Gupta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198940_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198940-35">[34]</a></sup> Some scholars, such as S. R. Goyal, theorise that the Guptas were <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmins" title="Brahmins">Brahmanas</a>, because they had matrimonial relations with Brahmans, but others reject this evidence as inconclusive.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198943–44_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198943–44-36">[35]</a></sup> Based on the Pune and Riddhapur inscriptions of the Gupta princess <a href="/wiki/Prabhavatigupta" title="Prabhavatigupta">Prabhavati-gupta</a>, some scholars believe that the name of her paternal <a href="/wiki/Gotra" title="Gotra">gotra</a> (clan) was "Dharana", but an alternative reading of these inscriptions suggests that Dharana was the <i>gotra</i> of her mother Kuberanaga.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198983_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198983-37">[36]</a></sup>
</p><p>However, recent excavations in Nepal and Deccan has revealed that Gupta suffix was common among <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abhira" title="Abhira">Abhira</a> kings, and Historian D. R. Regmi, links Imperial Guptas with <a href="/wiki/Abhira-Gupta_dynasty_(Nepal)" title="Abhira-Gupta dynasty (Nepal)">Abhira Guptas</a> of Nepal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[37]</a></sup> In Bhagavatam, Gupta dynasty has been called Abhir.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[38]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">For historical calendar era, see <a href="/wiki/Gupta_era" title="Gupta era">Gupta era</a>.</div>

<p><a href="/wiki/Gupta_(king)" title="Gupta (king)">Gupta</a> (<a href="/wiki/Gupta_script" title="Gupta script">Gupta script</a>: <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg"><img alt="Gupta allahabad gu.jpg" data-file-height="416" data-file-width="444" decoding="async" height="13" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg/14px-Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg/21px-Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7b/Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg/28px-Gupta_allahabad_gu.jpg 2x" width="14"/></a><sub><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg"><img alt="Gupta allahabad pt.jpg" data-file-height="567" data-file-width="366" decoding="async" height="19" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg/12px-Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg/18px-Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/11/Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg/24px-Gupta_allahabad_pt.jpg 2x" width="12"/></a></sub> <i>gu-pta</i>, fl. late 3rd century CE) is the earliest known king of the dynasty: different historians variously date the beginning of his reign from mid-to-late 3rd century CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198949–55_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198949–55-41">[40]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198986_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198986-42">[41]</a></sup> Sri Gupta founded the Gupta Empire c. 240-280 CE, and was succeeded by his son, Ghatotkacha, c. 280-319 CE, followed by Ghatotkacha’s son, Chandragupta, c. 319-335 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[42]</a></sup>"Che-li-ki-to", the name of a king mentioned by the 7th century Chinese Buddhist monk <a href="/wiki/Yijing_(monk)" title="Yijing (monk)">Yijing</a>, is believed to be a transcription of "<a href="/wiki/Shri" title="Shri">Shri</a>-Gupta" (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IAST" title="IAST">IAST</a>: Śrigupta), "Shri" being an honorific prefix.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198984–85_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198984–85-44">[43]</a></sup> According to Yijing, this king built a temple for Chinese Buddhist pilgrims near "Mi-li-kia-si-kia-po-no" (believed to be a transcription of <a href="/wiki/M%E1%B9%9Bga%C5%9Bikh%C4%81vana" title="Mṛgaśikhāvana">Mṛgaśikhāvana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198979–81_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198979–81-45">[44]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/Allahabad_Pillar" title="Allahabad Pillar">Allahabad Pillar</a> inscription, Gupta and his successor <a href="/wiki/Ghatotkacha_(king)" title="Ghatotkacha (king)">Ghatotkacha</a> are described as <i><a href="/wiki/Maharaja" title="Maharaja">Maharaja</a></i> ("great king"), while the next king <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_I" title="Chandragupta I">Chandragupta I</a> is called a <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maharajadhiraja" title="Maharajadhiraja">Maharajadhiraja</a></i> ("king of great kings"). In the later period, the title <i>Maharaja</i> was used by feudatory rulers, which has led to suggestions that Gupta and Ghatotkacha were vassals (possibly of <a href="/wiki/Kushan_Empire" title="Kushan Empire">Kushan Empire</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198985_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal198985-46">[45]</a></sup> However, there are several instances of paramount sovereigns using the title <i>Maharaja</i>, in both pre-Gupta and post-Gupta periods, so this cannot be said with certainty. That said, there is no doubt that Gupta and Ghatotkacha held a lower status and were less powerful than Chandragupta I.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar19816–7_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar19816–7-47">[46]</a></sup>
</p><p>Chandragupta I married the <a href="/wiki/Licchavi_(tribe)" title="Licchavi (tribe)">Licchavi</a> princess Kumaradevi, which may have helped him extend his political power and dominions, enabling him to adopt the imperial title <i>Maharajadhiraja</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198110_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198110-48">[47]</a></sup> According to the dynasty's official records, he was succeeded by his son <a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</a>. However, the discovery of the coins issued by a Gupta ruler named <a href="/wiki/Kacha_(king)" title="Kacha (king)">Kacha</a> have led to some debate on this topic: according to one theory, Kacha was another name for Samudragupta; another possibility is that Kacha was a rival claimant to the throne.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198971_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198971-49">[48]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Samudragupta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</a> succeeded his father around 335 or 350 CE, and ruled until c. 375 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198951–52_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198951–52-50">[49]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Allahabad_Pillar" title="Allahabad Pillar">Allahabad Pillar</a> inscription, composed by his courtier <a href="/wiki/Harisena" title="Harisena">Harishena</a>, credits him with extensive conquests.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989106–07_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989106–07-51">[50]</a></sup> The inscription asserts that Samudragupta uprooted 8 kings of <a href="/wiki/%C4%80ry%C4%81varta" title="Āryāvarta">Āryāvarta</a>, the northern region, including the <a href="/wiki/Nagas_of_Padmavati" title="Nagas of Padmavati">Nagas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989114_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989114-52">[51]</a></sup> It further claims that he subjugated all the kings of the forest region, which was most probably located in central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989117_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989117-53">[52]</a></sup> It also credits him with defeating 12 rulers of <a href="/wiki/Dakshinapatha" title="Dakshinapatha">Dakshinapatha</a>, the southern region: the exact identification of several of these kings is debated among modern scholars,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989107_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989107-54">[53]</a></sup> but it is clear that these kings ruled areas located on the eastern coast of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112-55">[54]</a></sup> The inscription suggests that Samudragupta advanced as far as the <a href="/wiki/Pallava_dynasty" title="Pallava dynasty">Pallava</a> kingdom in the south, and defeated Vishnugopa, the Pallava regent of <a href="/wiki/Kanchipuram" title="Kanchipuram">Kanchi</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989110_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989110-56">[55]</a></sup> During this southern campaign, Samudragupta most probably passed through the forest tract of central India, reached the eastern coast in present-day <a href="/wiki/Odisha" title="Odisha">Odisha</a>, and then marched south along the coast of the <a href="/wiki/Bay_of_Bengal" title="Bay of Bengal">Bay of Bengal</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198980–81_57-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198980–81-57">[56]</a></sup>
</p>

<div id="annotation_100x165" style="position:absolute; left:100px; top:165px; font-size:8px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:10px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vakatakas" title="Vakatakas">VAKATAKAS</a></span></div>

<div id="annotation_247x90" style="position:absolute; left:247px; top:90px; font-size:6px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:8px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Varman_dynasty" title="Varman dynasty">KAMARUPAS</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_45x20" style="position:absolute; left:45px; top:20px; font-size:7px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:9px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Alchon_Huns" title="Alchon Huns">ALCHON HUNS</a></span></div>

<div id="annotation_1x45" style="position:absolute; left:1px; top:45px; font-size:8px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:10px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">SASANIAN<br/>EMPIRE</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_270x190" style="position:absolute; left:270px; top:190px; font-size:5px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:7px; text-align:right;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;">MAPS<br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_500_BCE" title="Template:South Asia in 500 BCE">-500</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_150_BCE" title="Template:South Asia in 150 BCE">-150</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_120_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 120 CE">120</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_350_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 350 CE">350</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_500_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 500 CE">500</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_600_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 600 CE">600</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1000" title="Template:South Asia in 1000">1000</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1175" title="Template:South Asia in 1175">1175</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1250" title="Template:South Asia in 1250">1250</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1400" title="Template:South Asia in 1400">1400</a></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png"><img alt="" data-file-height="2151" data-file-width="2324" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png/290px-Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png/435px-Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/34/Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png/580px-Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png 2x" width="290"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Map_of_the_Gupta_Empire.png" title="File:Map of the Gupta Empire.png"> </a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Map of the Gupta Empire circa 400-450 CE, according to <a href="/wiki/Joseph_E._Schwartzberg" title="Joseph E. Schwartzberg">Joseph E. Schwartzberg</a>, with contemporary polities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[57]</a></sup></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>The Allahabad Pillar inscription mentions that rulers of several frontier kingdoms and tribal <a href="/wiki/Oligarchy" title="Oligarchy">oligarchies</a> paid Samudragupta tributes, obeyed his orders, and performed obeisance before him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198984_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198984-59">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343-10">[9]</a></sup> The frontier kingdoms included <a href="/wiki/Samatata" title="Samatata">Samatata</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Davaka" title="Davaka">Davaka</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kamarupa" title="Kamarupa">Kamarupa</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nepala" title="Nepala">Nepala</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Katyuri_kings" title="Katyuri kings">Karttripura</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112–18_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112–18-9">[8]</a></sup> The tribal oligarchies included <a href="/wiki/Malavas" title="Malavas">Malavas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arjunayanas" title="Arjunayanas">Arjunayanas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yaudheyas" title="Yaudheyas">Yaudheyas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Madra_Kingdom" title="Madra Kingdom">Madrakas</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Abhira_tribe" title="Abhira tribe">Abhiras</a>, among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343_10-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUpinder_Singh2017343-10">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>Finally, the inscription mentions that several foreign kings tried to please Samudragupta  by personal attendance; offered him their daughters in marriage (or according to another interpretation, gifted him maidens<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989125_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989125-60">[59]</a></sup>); and sought the use of the <a href="/wiki/Garuda" title="Garuda">Garuda</a>-depicting Gupta seal for administering their own territories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEShankar_Goyal2001168_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEShankar_Goyal2001168-61">[60]</a></sup> This is an exaggeration: for example, the inscription lists the king of <a href="/wiki/Sri_Lanka" title="Sri Lanka">Simhala</a> among these kings. It is known that from Chinese sources that the Simhala king <a href="/wiki/Sirimeghavanna_of_Anuradhapura" title="Sirimeghavanna of Anuradhapura">Meghavarna</a> sent rich presents to the Gupta king requesting his permission to build a Buddhist monastery at <a href="/wiki/Bodh_Gaya" title="Bodh Gaya">Bodh Gaya</a>: Samudragupta's panegyrist appears to have described this act of diplomacy as an act of subservience.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198990_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198990-62">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p>Samudragupta appears to have been <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vaishnavite" title="Vaishnavite">Vaishnavite</a>, as attested by his Eran inscription,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198968_63-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198968-63">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198132_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198132-64">[63]</a></sup> and performed several <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmanism" title="Brahmanism">Brahmanical</a> ceremonies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198991_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198991-65">[64]</a></sup> The Gupta records credit him with making generous donations of cows and gold.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198968_63-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198968-63">[62]</a></sup> He performed the <a href="/wiki/Ashvamedha" title="Ashvamedha">Ashvamedha</a> ritual (horse sacrifice), which was used by the ancient Indian kings to prove their imperial sovereignty, and issued gold coins (see <a href="#Coinage">Coinage</a> below) to mark this performance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989125–26_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989125–26-66">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Allahabad Pillar inscription presents Samudragupta as a wise king and strict administrator, who was also compassionate enough to help the poor and the helpless.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198991,_94_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198991,_94-67">[66]</a></sup> It also alludes to the king's talents as a musician and a poet, and calls him the "king of poets".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198131_68-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198131-68">[67]</a></sup> Such claims are corroborated by Samudragupta's gold coins, which depict him playing a <a href="/wiki/Ancient_veena" title="Ancient veena">veena</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198994_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETej_Ram_Sharma198994-69">[68]</a></sup>
</p><p>Samudragupta appears to have directly controlled a large part of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Gangetic_Plain" title="Indo-Gangetic Plain">Indo-Gangetic Plain</a> in present-day India, as well as a substantial part of central India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198123,_27_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198123,_27-70">[69]</a></sup> Besides, his empire comprised a number of monarchical and tribal tributary states of northern India, and of the south-eastern coastal region of India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198122_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTER.C._Majumdar198122-71">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112_55-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989112-55">[54]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ramagupta">Ramagupta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Ramagupta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5550" data-file-width="3053" decoding="async" height="309" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg/170px-Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg/255px-Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg/340px-Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Standing_Buddha_Installed_by_Buddist_Monk_Yasadinna_-_Circa_5th_Century_CE_-_Jamalpur_Mound_-_ACCN_00-A-5_-_Government_Museum_Mathura_Golden_background.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Standing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a> in red sandstone, <a href="/wiki/Art_of_Mathura" title="Art of Mathura">Art of Mathura</a>, Gupta period circa 5th century CE. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mathura_Museum" title="Mathura Museum">Mathura Museum</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[71]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Ramagupta" title="Ramagupta">Ramagupta</a></div>
<p>Ramagupta is known from a sixth-century play, the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Devichandragupta" title="Devichandragupta">Devichandragupta</a></i>, in which he surrenders his wife to the enemy <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Sakas</a> and his brother Chandragupta has to sneak into the enemy camp to rescue her and kill the Saka king. The historicity of these events is unclear, but Ramagupta's existence is confirmed by three <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jain</a> statues found at Durjanpur, with inscriptions referring to him as the <i>Maharajadhiraja</i>. A large number of his copper coins also have been found from the <a href="/wiki/Eran" title="Eran">Eran</a>-<a href="/wiki/Vidisha" title="Vidisha">Vidisha</a> region and classified in five distinct types, which include the <i>Garuda</i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989153–59_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989153–59-73">[72]</a></sup> <i>Garudadhvaja</i>, <i>lion</i> and <i>border legend</i> types. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahmi" title="Brahmi">Brahmi</a> legends on these coins are written in the early Gupta style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[73]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Chandragupta_II_.22Vikramaditya.22"></span><span class="mw-headline" id='Chandragupta_II_"Vikramaditya"'>Chandragupta II "Vikramaditya"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title='Edit section: Chandragupta II "Vikramaditya"'>edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a></div>
<p>According to the Gupta records, amongst his sons, Samudragupta nominated prince Chandragupta II, born of queen <a href="/wiki/Dattadevi" title="Dattadevi">Dattadevi</a>, as his successor. Chandragupta II, <i>Vikramaditya</i> (Victory of the Sun), ruled from 375 until 415. He married a Kadamba princess of Kuntala and of Naga lineage (<i>Nāgakulotpannnā</i>), Kuberanaga. His daughter <a href="/wiki/Prabhavatigupta" title="Prabhavatigupta">Prabhavatigupta</a> from this Naga queen was married to <a href="/wiki/Rudrasena_II" title="Rudrasena II">Rudrasena II</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vakataka" title="Vakataka">Vakataka</a> ruler of <a href="/wiki/Deccan_Plateau" title="Deccan Plateau">Deccan</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923489_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923489-75">[74]</a></sup> His son Kumaragupta I was married to a Kadamba princess of the Karnataka region. Chandragupta II expanded his realm westwards, defeating the <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Saka</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Kshatrapas" title="Western Kshatrapas">Western Kshatrapas</a> of <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> and <a href="/wiki/Saurashtra_(region)" title="Saurashtra (region)">Saurashtra</a> in a campaign lasting until 409. His main opponent <a href="/wiki/Rudrasimha_III" title="Rudrasimha III">Rudrasimha III</a> was defeated by 395, and he crushed the Bengal chiefdoms. This extended his control from coast to coast, established a second capital at <a href="/wiki/Ujjain" title="Ujjain">Ujjain</a> and was the high point of the empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Kuntala inscriptions indicate rule of Chandragupta in <a href="/wiki/Kuntala_country" title="Kuntala country">Kuntala region</a> of Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Karnataka" title="Karnataka">Karnataka</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[75]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sacred_Rock_of_Hunza" title="Sacred Rock of Hunza">Hunza inscription</a> also indicate that Chandragupta was able to rule north western Indian subcontinent and proceeded to conquer <a href="/wiki/Balkh" title="Balkh">Balkh</a>, although some scholars have also disputed the identity of gupta king.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[77]</a></sup> Chalukyan ruler <a href="/wiki/Vikramaditya_VI" title="Vikramaditya VI">Vikramditya VI</a> (r. 1076 – 1126 CE) mentions Chandragupta with his title and states 'why should the glory of the Kings Vikramaditya and Nanda be a hindrance any longer ? he with a loud command abolished that (era), which has the name of Saka, and made that (era) which has the Chalukya counting ” <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[78]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="1018" decoding="async" height="110" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg/220px-Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg/330px-Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9b/Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg/440px-Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Two_Gold_coins_of_Chandragupta_II.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold coins of <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>Despite the creation of the empire through war, the reign is remembered for its very influential style of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> art, literature, culture and science, especially during the reign of Chandragupta II. Some excellent works of Hindu art such as the panels at the Dashavatara Temple in <a href="/wiki/Deogarh,_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Deogarh, Uttar Pradesh">Deogarh</a> serve to illustrate the magnificence of Gupta art. Above all, it was the synthesis of elements that gave Gupta art its distinctive flavour. During this period, the Guptas were supportive of thriving <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> cultures as well, and for this reason, there is also a long history of non-Hindu <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gupta_period_art" title="Gupta period art">Gupta period art</a>. In particular, Gupta period Buddhist art was to be influential in most of East and Southeast Asia. Many advances were recorded by the Chinese scholar and traveller <a href="/wiki/Faxian" title="Faxian">Faxian</a> in his diary and published afterwards.
</p><p>The court of Chandragupta was made even more illustrious by the fact that it was graced by the <i>Navaratna</i> (Nine Jewels), a group of nine who excelled in the literary arts. Amongst these men was <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/K%C4%81lid%C4%81sa" title="Kālidāsa">Kālidāsa</a>, whose works dwarfed the works of many other literary geniuses, not only in his own age but in the years to come. Kalidasa was mainly known for his subtle exploitation of the <i>shringara</i> (romantic) element in his verse.
</p>
<h4><span id="Chandragupta_II.27s_campaigns_against_foreign_tribes"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Chandragupta_II's_campaigns_against_foreign_tribes">Chandragupta II's campaigns against foreign tribes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Chandragupta II's campaigns against foreign tribes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vishnu_sculpture.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4000" data-file-width="3000" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vishnu_sculpture.jpg/170px-Vishnu_sculpture.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vishnu_sculpture.jpg/255px-Vishnu_sculpture.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Vishnu_sculpture.jpg/340px-Vishnu_sculpture.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vishnu_sculpture.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sculpture of Vishnu (red sandstone), 5th century CE.</div></div></div>
<p>The 4th century <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a> credits Chandragupta Vikramaditya with conquering about twenty-one kingdoms, both in and outside India. After finishing his campaign in East and West India, Vikramaditya (Chandragupta II) proceeded northwards, subjugated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasika" title="Parasika">Parasikas</a>, then the <a href="/wiki/Huna_people" title="Huna people">Hunas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a> tribes located in the west and east <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxus" title="Oxus">Oxus</a> valleys respectively. Thereafter, the king proceeded into the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Himalaya" title="Himalaya">Himalaya</a> mountains to reduce the mountain tribes of the <a href="/wiki/Kinnara_Kingdom" title="Kinnara Kingdom">Kinnaras</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kiratas" title="Kiratas">Kiratas</a>, as well as India proper.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raghu_Vamsa_v_4.60–75_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raghu_Vamsa_v_4.60–75-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (August 2016)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In one of his works Kalidasa also credits him with the removal of the <a href="/wiki/Saka" title="Saka">Sakas</a> from the country. He wrote 'Wasn't it Vikramaditya who drove the Sakas out from the lovely city of Ujjain?'.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[79]</a></sup>
</p><p>The <i>Brihatkathamanjari</i> of the <a href="/wiki/Kashmir" title="Kashmir">Kashmiri</a> writer <a href="/wiki/Kshemendra" title="Kshemendra">Kshemendra</a> states, King Vikramaditya (Chandragupta II) had "unburdened the sacred earth of the <a href="/wiki/Barbarian" title="Barbarian">Barbarians</a> like the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sakas" title="Sakas">Sakas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mleccha" title="Mleccha">Mlecchas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kambojas" title="Kambojas">Kambojas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yavana" title="Yavana">Yavanas</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tushara_Kingdom" title="Tushara Kingdom">Tusharas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persians" title="Persians">Parasikas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Huna_people" title="Huna people">Hunas</a>, and others, by annihilating these sinful Mlecchas completely".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[80]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:No_original_research#Primary,_secondary_and_tertiary_sources" title="Wikipedia:No original research"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable secondary sources. (August 2016)">non-primary source needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[82]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Reliable sources"><span title="The material near this tag may rely on an unreliable source. (August 2016)">unreliable source?</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Faxian">Faxian</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Faxian">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p><a href="/wiki/Faxian" title="Faxian">Faxian</a> (or Fa Hsien etc.), a Chinese <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhist" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a>, was one of the pilgrims who visited India during the reign of the Gupta emperor <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a>. He started his journey from China in 399 and reached India in 405. During his stay in India up to 411, he went on a pilgrimage to <a href="/wiki/Mathura" title="Mathura">Mathura</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kannauj" title="Kannauj">Kannauj</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kapilavastu_(ancient_city)" title="Kapilavastu (ancient city)">Kapilavastu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kushinagar" title="Kushinagar">Kushinagar</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vaishali_(ancient_city)" title="Vaishali (ancient city)">Vaishali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varanasi" title="Varanasi">Kashi</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rajagriha" title="Rajagriha">Rajagriha</a>, and made careful observations about the empire's conditions. Faxian was pleased with the mildness of administration. The Penal Code was mild and offences were punished by fines only. From his accounts, the Gupta Empire was a prosperous period. His writings form one of the most important sources for the history of this period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fa-hsien_1886_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fa-hsien_1886-84">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Faxian" title="Faxian">Faxian</a> on reaching <a href="/wiki/Mathura" title="Mathura">Mathura</a> comments––</p><blockquote><p>"The snow and heat are finely tempered, and there is neither hoarfrost nor snow. The people are numerous and happy. They have not to register their households. Only those who cultivate the royal land have to pay (a portion of) the gain from it. If they want to go, they go. If they want to stay on, they stay on. The king governs without decapitation or (other) corporal punishments. Criminals are simply fined according to circumstances. Even in cases of repeated attempts at wicked rebellion, they only have their right-hand cut off. The king's bodyguards &amp; attendants all have salaries. Throughout the whole country, the people do not kill any living creature, not drink any intoxicating liquor, nor eat onions or garlic."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fa-hsien_1886_84-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fa-hsien_1886-84">[83]</a></sup></p></blockquote>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Kumaragupta_I">Kumaragupta I</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Kumaragupta I">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_I" title="Kumaragupta I">Kumaragupta I</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1258" data-file-width="2822" decoding="async" height="147" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg/330px-Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg/495px-Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg/660px-Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Silver_Coin_of_Kumaragupta_I.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Silver coin of the Gupta King <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_I" title="Kumaragupta I">Kumaragupta I</a> (Coin of his Western territories, design derived from the <a href="/wiki/Western_Satraps" title="Western Satraps">Western Satraps</a>). <br/><i>Obv</i>: Bust of king with crescents, with traces of corrupt Greek script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[84]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[85]</a></sup> <br/><i>Rev</i>: <a href="/wiki/Garuda" title="Garuda">Garuda</a> standing facing with spread wings. Brahmi legend: <i>Parama-bhagavata <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rajadhiraja" title="Rajadhiraja">rajadhiraja</a> Sri Kumaragupta Mahendraditya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[86]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Chandragupta II was succeeded by his second son <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumara_Gupta_I" title="Kumara Gupta I">Kumaragupta I</a>, born of <i>Mahadevi</i> Dhruvasvamini. Kumaragupta I assumed the title, <i>Mahendraditya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989191–200_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAshvini_Agrawal1989191–200-88">[87]</a></sup> He ruled until 455. Towards the end of his reign a tribe in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narmada" title="Narmada">Narmada</a> valley, the <a href="/wiki/Pushyamitras" title="Pushyamitras">Pushyamitras</a>, rose in power to threaten the empire. The <a href="/wiki/Kidarites" title="Kidarites">Kidarites</a> as well probably confronted the Gupta Empire towards the end of the rule of Kumaragupta I, as his son <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a> mentions in the <a href="/wiki/Bhitari_pillar_inscription_of_Skandagupta" title="Bhitari pillar inscription of Skandagupta">Bhitari pillar inscription</a> his efforts at reshaping a country in disarray, through reorganisation and military victories over the <a href="/wiki/Pushyamitras" title="Pushyamitras">Pushyamitras</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunas" title="Hunas">Hunas</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HCCE_119_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-HCCE_119-89">[88]</a></sup>
</p><p>He was the founder of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nalanda_(university)" title="Nalanda (university)">Nalanda University</a> which on 15 July 2016 was declared as a UNESCO world heritage site.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[89]</a></sup> Kumaragupta I was also a worshipper of <a href="/wiki/Kartikeya" title="Kartikeya">Kartikeya</a>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Skandagupta">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a>, son and successor of Kumaragupta I is generally considered to be the last of the great Gupta rulers. He assumed the titles of <i>Vikramaditya</i> and <i>Kramaditya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923510_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923510-91">[90]</a></sup> He defeated the Pushyamitra threat, but then was faced with invading <a href="/wiki/Kidarites" title="Kidarites">Kidarites</a> (sometimes described as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hephthalite" title="Hephthalite">Hephthalites</a> or "White Huns", known in India as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Huna_(people)" title="Huna (people)">Sweta Huna</a>), from the northwest.
</p><p>He repelled a <i>Huna</i> attack around 455 CE, but the expense of the wars drained the empire's resources and contributed to its decline. The Bhitari Pillar inscription of <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a>, the successor of Chandragupta, recalls the near-annihilation of the Gupta Empire following the attacks of the <a href="/wiki/Kidarites" title="Kidarites">Kidarites</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyun_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyun-92">[91]</a></sup> The Kidarites seem to have retained the western part of the Gupta Empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyun_92-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hyun-92">[91]</a></sup>
</p><p>Skandagupta died in 467 and was succeeded by his agnate brother <a href="/wiki/Purugupta" title="Purugupta">Purugupta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923516_93-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEH.C._Raychaudhuri1923516-93">[92]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Decline_of_the_empire">Decline of the empire</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Decline of the empire">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kakandi.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="960" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Kakandi.jpg/170px-Kakandi.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Kakandi.jpg/255px-Kakandi.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Kakandi.jpg/340px-Kakandi.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Kakandi.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> <a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">tirthankara</a> relief <a href="/wiki/Parshvanatha" title="Parshvanatha">Parshvanatha</a> on <a href="/wiki/Kahaum_pillar" title="Kahaum pillar">Kahaum pillar</a> erected by Skandagupta<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[93]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Following Skandagupta's death, the empire was clearly in decline,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[94]</a></sup> and the later Gupta coinage indicates their loss of control over much of western India after 467–469.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gupta_Dynasty_–_MSN_Encarta_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gupta_Dynasty_–_MSN_Encarta-4">[4]</a></sup> Skandagupta was followed by <a href="/wiki/Purugupta" title="Purugupta">Purugupta</a> (467–473), <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_II" title="Kumaragupta II">Kumaragupta II</a> (473–476), <a href="/wiki/Budhagupta" title="Budhagupta">Budhagupta</a> (476–495), <a href="/wiki/Narasimhagupta" title="Narasimhagupta">Narasimhagupta</a> (495—530), <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_III" title="Kumaragupta III">Kumaragupta III</a> (530—540), <a href="/wiki/Vishnugupta_(Gupta_Empire)" title="Vishnugupta (Gupta Empire)">Vishnugupta</a> (540—550), two lesser known kings namely, <a href="/wiki/Vainyagupta" title="Vainyagupta">Vainyagupta</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bhanugupta" title="Bhanugupta">Bhanugupta</a>.
</p><p>In the 480's the <a href="/wiki/Alchon_Huns" title="Alchon Huns">Alchon Huns</a> under <a href="/wiki/Toramana" title="Toramana">Toramana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mihirakula" title="Mihirakula">Mihirakula</a> broke through the Gupta defences in the northwest, and much of the empire in the northwest was overrun by the Huns by 500. According to some scholars the empire disintegrated under the attacks of <a href="/wiki/Toramana" title="Toramana">Toramana</a> and his successor <a href="/wiki/Mihirakula" title="Mihirakula">Mihirakula</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[95]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[96]</a></sup> It appears from inscriptions that the Guptas, although their power was much diminished, continued to resist the Huns. The Hun invader Toramana was defeated by <a href="/wiki/Bhanugupta" title="Bhanugupta">Bhanugupta</a> in 510.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[97]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[98]</a></sup> The Huns were defeated and driven out of India in 528 by King <a href="/wiki/Yashodharman" title="Yashodharman">Yashodharman</a> from <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a>, and possibly Gupta emperor <a href="/wiki/Narasimhagupta" title="Narasimhagupta">Narasimhagupta</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Columbia_Encyclopedia_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Columbia_Encyclopedia-100">[99]</a></sup>
</p><p>These invasions, although only spanning a few decades, had long term effects on India, and in a sense brought an end to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_India" title="Classical India">Classical Indian civilisation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-101">[100]</a></sup> Soon after the invasions, the Gupta Empire, already weakened by these invasions and the rise of local rulers such as <a href="/wiki/Yashodharman" title="Yashodharman">Yashodharman</a>, ended as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[101]</a></sup> Following the invasions, northern India was left in disarray, with numerous smaller Indian powers emerging after the crumbling of the Guptas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[102]</a></sup> The Huna invasions are said to have seriously damaged India's trade with <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Central_Asia" title="Central Asia">Central Asia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_101-1"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-101">[100]</a></sup> In particular, <a href="/wiki/Indo-Roman_trade_relations" title="Indo-Roman trade relations">Indo-Roman trade relations</a>, which the Gupta Empire had greatly benefited from. The Guptas had been exporting numerous luxury products such as <a href="/wiki/Silk" title="Silk">silk</a>, leather goods, fur, iron products, <a href="/wiki/Ivory" title="Ivory">ivory</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pearl" title="Pearl">pearl</a>, and pepper from centres such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nasik" title="Nasik">Nasik</a>, <a href="/wiki/Paithan" title="Paithan">Paithan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pataliputra" title="Pataliputra">Pataliputra</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Benares" title="Benares">Benares</a>. The Huna invasion probably disrupted these trade relations and the tax revenues that came with them.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Furthermore, Indian urban culture was left in decline, and <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>, gravely weakened by the destruction of monasteries and the killing of monks by the hand of the vehemently anti-Buddhist <a href="/wiki/Shaivism" title="Shaivism">Shaivist</a> Mihirakula, started to collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_101-2"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-101">[100]</a></sup> Great centres of learning were destroyed, such as the city of <a href="/wiki/Taxila" title="Taxila">Taxila</a>, bringing cultural regression.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_101-3"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-101">[100]</a></sup> During their rule of 60 years, the Alchons are said to have altered the hierarchy of ruling families and the Indian <a href="/wiki/Caste_system_in_India" title="Caste system in India">caste system</a>. For example, the Hunas are often said to have become the precursors of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rajputs" title="Rajputs">Rajputs</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Eraly_101-4"><a href="#cite_note-Eraly-101">[100]</a></sup>
</p>
