<div id="annotation_100x160" style="position:absolute; left:100px; top:160px; font-size:7px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:9px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Chalukya_dynasty" title="Chalukya dynasty">CHALUKYAS</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_105x140" style="position:absolute; left:105px; top:140px; font-size:7px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:9px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Kalachuri_dynasty" title="Kalachuri dynasty">KALACHURIS</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_5x55" style="position:absolute; left:5px; top:55px; font-size:8.5px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:10.5px; text-align:center;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;"><a href="/wiki/Sasanian_Empire" title="Sasanian Empire">SASANIAN<br/>EMPIRE</a></span></div>
<div id="annotation_270x175" style="position:absolute; left:270px; top:175px; font-size:5px; font-weight:bold; font-style:normal; line-height:7px; text-align:right;"><span style="background-color:transparent; color:#000000;">MAPS<br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_500_BCE" title="Template:South Asia in 500 BCE">-500</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_150_BCE" title="Template:South Asia in 150 BCE">-150</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_120_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 120 CE">120</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_350_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 350 CE">350</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_500_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 500 CE">500</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_600_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 600 CE">600</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_800_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 800 CE">800</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1000" title="Template:South Asia in 1000">1000</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1175" title="Template:South Asia in 1175">1175</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1250" title="Template:South Asia in 1250">1250</a><br/><a href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1400" title="Template:South Asia in 1400">1400</a><br/><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Template:South_Asia_in_1500_CE" title="Template:South Asia in 1500 CE">1500</a></span></div>
</div>
<div style="visibility:hidden"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_non_political,_with_rivers.jpg"><img alt="" data-file-height="2151" data-file-width="2324" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/290px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/435px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg/580px-South_Asia_non_political%2C_with_rivers.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a></div>
</div>
<div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:South_Asia_non_political,_with_rivers.jpg" title="File:South Asia non political, with rivers.jpg"> </a></div><div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">Political fragmentation of South Asia after the retreat of the <a href="/wiki/Alchon_Huns" title="Alchon Huns">Alchon Huns</a> to the northwest and the end of the Gupta Empire, circa 600 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[104]</a></sup></div></div>
</div></div>
<p>The succession of the 6th-century Guptas is not entirely clear, but the tail end recognised ruler of the dynasty's main line was king <a href="/wiki/Vishnugupta_(Gupta_Empire)" title="Vishnugupta (Gupta Empire)">Vishnugupta</a>, reigning from 540 to 550. In addition to the Hun invasion, the factors, which contribute to the decline of the empire include competition from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vakatakas" title="Vakatakas">Vakatakas</a> and the rise of <a href="/wiki/Yashodharman" title="Yashodharman">Yashodharman</a> in <a href="/wiki/Malwa" title="Malwa">Malwa</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-singh3_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-singh3-106">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p>The last known inscription by a Gupta emperor is from the reign of Vishnugupta (the Damodarpur copper-plate inscription),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[106]</a></sup> in which he makes a land grant in the area of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kotivarsha" title="Kotivarsha">Kotivarsha</a> (<a href="/wiki/Bangarh" title="Bangarh">Bangarh</a> in <a href="/wiki/West_Bengal" title="West Bengal">West Bengal</a>) in 542/543 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-108">[107]</a></sup> This follows the occupation of most of northern and central India by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aulikara" title="Aulikara">Aulikara</a> ruler <a href="/wiki/Yashodharman" title="Yashodharman">Yashodharman</a> circa 532 CE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Davidson_108-1"><a href="#cite_note-Davidson-108">[107]</a></sup>
</p><p>A 2019 study by archaeologist Shanker Sharma has concluded that the cause of the Gupta empire's downfall was a devastating flood which happened around the middle of the 6th century in <a href="/wiki/Uttar_Pradesh" title="Uttar Pradesh">Uttar Pradesh</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bihar" title="Bihar">Bihar</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Gupta_successor_dynasties">Post-Gupta successor dynasties</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-Gupta successor dynasties">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the heart of the former Gupta Empire, in the Gangetic region, the Guptas were succeeded by the <a href="/wiki/Maukhari_dynasty" title="Maukhari dynasty">Maukhari dynasty</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pushyabhuti_dynasty" title="Pushyabhuti dynasty">Pushyabhuti dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPR_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-HPR-110">[109]</a></sup> The coinage of the Maukharis and Pushyabhutis followed the silver coin type of the Guptas, with portrait of the ruler in profile (although facing in the reverse direction compared to the Guptas, a possible symbol of antagonism)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RST_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-RST-111">[110]</a></sup> and the peacock on the reverse, the Brahmi legend being kept except for the name of the ruler.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPR_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-HPR-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the western regions, they were succeeded by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gurjara" title="Gurjara">Gurjaras</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pratiharas" title="Pratiharas">Pratiharas</a>, and later the <a href="/wiki/Chaulukya_dynasty" title="Chaulukya dynasty">Chaulukya</a>-<a href="/wiki/Paramara_dynasty" title="Paramara dynasty">Paramara</a> dynasties, who issued so-called <a href="/wiki/Indo-Sasanian_coinage" title="Indo-Sasanian coinage">Indo-Sasanian coinage</a>, on the model of the <a href="/wiki/Sasanian_coinage" title="Sasanian coinage">coinage of the Sasanian Empire</a>, which had been introduced in India by the Alchon Huns.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-HPR_110-2"><a href="#cite_note-HPR-110">[109]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Military_organisation">Military organisation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Military organisation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="948" data-file-width="952" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/220px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/330px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/440px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An 8 gm gold coin featuring <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a> astride a caparisoned horse with a bow in his left hand.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[111]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>In contrast to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mauryan_Empire" title="Mauryan Empire">Mauryan Empire</a>, the Guptas introduced several military innovations to Indian warfare. Chief amongst these was the use of <a href="/wiki/Siege_engine" title="Siege engine">Siege engines</a>, heavy cavalry archers and heavy sword cavalry. The <a href="/wiki/Heavy_cavalry" title="Heavy cavalry">heavy cavalry</a> formed the core of the Gupta army and were supported by the traditional Indian army elements of elephants and <a href="/wiki/Light_infantry" title="Light infantry">light infantry</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[112]</a></sup>
</p><p>The utilisation of horse archers in the Gupta period is evidenced on the coinage of <a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandragupta II</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_I" title="Kumaragupta I">Kumaragupta I</a> and Prakasaditya (postulated to be <a href="/wiki/Purugupta" title="Purugupta">Purugupta</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[113]</a></sup> that depicts the emperors as horse-archers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>Unfortunately there is a paucity of contemporary sources detailing the tactical operations of the Imperial Gupta Army. The best extant information comes from the Sanskrit mahakavya (epic poem) <a href="/wiki/Raghuva%E1%B9%83%C5%9Ba" title="Raghuvaṃśa">Raghuvaṃśa</a> written by the Classical Sanskrit writer and dramatist <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a>. Many modern scholars put forward the view that Kalidasa lived from the reign of Chandragupta II to the reign of <a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skandagupta</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[116]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[118]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-120"><a href="#cite_note-120">[119]</a></sup> and that the campaigns of Raghu – his protagonist in the Raghuvaṃśa – reflect those of Chandragupta II.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[120]</a></sup> In Canto IV of the Raghuvamsa, Kalidasa relates how the king's forces clash against the powerful, cavalry-centric, forces of the Persians and later the Yavanas (probably Huns) in the North-West. Here he makes special mention of the use horse-archers in the kings army and that the horses needed much rest after the hotly contested battles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[121]</a></sup> The five arms of the Gupta military included infantry, cavalry, chariot, elephants and ships. Gunaighar copper plate inscription of <a href="/wiki/Vainyagupta" title="Vainyagupta">Vainya Gupta</a> mentions ships but not chariots.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[122]</a></sup> Ships had become integral part of Indian military in the 6th century AD.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Religion">Religion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Religion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_(Dhammajak_Mutra).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1521" data-file-width="1188" decoding="async" height="218" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg/170px-Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg/255px-Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg/340px-Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_%28Dhammajak_Mutra%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha_in_Sarnath_Museum_(Dhammajak_Mutra).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmachakra_Pravartana_Buddha_at_Sarnath" title="Dharmachakra Pravartana Buddha at Sarnath">Dharmachakra Pravartana Buddha at Sarnath</a> from the Gupta era, 5th century CE.</div></div></div>
<p>The Guptas were traditionally a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindu" title="Hindu">Hindu</a> dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_124-0"><a href="#cite_note-Singh-124">[123]</a></sup> They were orthodox <a href="/wiki/Hindus" title="Hindus">Hindus</a>, and allowed followers of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jainism" title="Jainism">Jainism</a> to practice their religions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-125">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Sanchi" title="Sanchi">Sanchi</a> remained an important centre of Buddhism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_125-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-125">[124]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_I" title="Kumaragupta I">Kumaragupta I</a> (455 CE) is said to have founded <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mookerji_125-2"><a href="#cite_note-Mookerji-125">[124]</a></sup>  Modern genetic studies indicate that it was during the Gupta period that Indian caste groups ceased to intermarry (started practicing/enforcing <a href="/wiki/Endogamy" title="Endogamy">endogamy</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[125]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some later rulers however seem to have especially promoted <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Narasimhagupta_Baladitya" title="Narasimhagupta Baladitya">Narasimhagupta Baladitya</a> (c. 495–?), according to contemporary writer <a href="/wiki/Paramartha" title="Paramartha">Paramartha</a>, was brought up under the influence of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mahayanist" title="Mahayanist">Mahayanist</a> philosopher, <a href="/wiki/Vasubandhu" title="Vasubandhu">Vasubandhu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_124-1"><a href="#cite_note-Singh-124">[123]</a></sup> He built a <a href="/wiki/Sangharama" title="Sangharama">sangharama</a> at <a href="/wiki/Nalanda" title="Nalanda">Nalanda</a> and also a 300 ft (91 m) high <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vihara" title="Vihara">vihara</a> with a Buddha statue within which, according to Xuanzang, resembled the "great Vihara built under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bodhi_tree" title="Bodhi tree">Bodhi tree</a>". According to the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Manjushrimulakalpa" title="Manjushrimulakalpa">Manjushrimulakalpa</a></i> (c. 800 CE), king Narasimhsagupta became a Buddhist monk, and left the world through meditation (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dhy%C4%81na_in_Buddhism" title="Dhyāna in Buddhism">Dhyana</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Singh_124-2"><a href="#cite_note-Singh-124">[123]</a></sup> The Chinese monk <a href="/wiki/Xuanzang" title="Xuanzang">Xuanzang</a> also noted that Narasimhagupta Baladitya's son, Vajra, who commissioned a sangharama as well, "possessed a heart firm in faith".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sankalia_127-0"><a href="#cite_note-sankalia-127">[126]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 45">: 45 </span></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-sdutt_128-0"><a href="#cite_note-sdutt-128">[127]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 330">: 330 </span></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Gupta_administration">Gupta administration</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Gupta administration">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>A study of the epigraphical records of the Gupta empire shows that there was a hierarchy of administrative divisions from top to bottom. The empire was called by various names such as <i>Rajya</i>, <i>Rashtra</i>, <i>Desha</i>, <i>Mandala</i>, <i>Prithvi</i> and <i>Avani</i>. It was divided into 26 provinces, which were styled as <i>Bhukti</i>, <i>Pradesha</i> and <i>Bhoga</i>. Provinces were also divided into <i>Vishayas</i> and put under the control of the <i>Vishayapati</i>s. A <i>Vishayapati</i> administered the <i>Vishaya</i> with the help of the <i>Adhikarana</i> (council of representatives), which comprised four representatives: <i>Nagarasreshesthi</i>, <i>Sarthavaha</i>, <i>Prathamakulika</i> and <i>Prathama Kayastha</i>. A part of the <i>Vishaya</i> was called <i>Vithi</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVidya_Dhar_Mahajan1990530–31_129-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVidya_Dhar_Mahajan1990530–31-129">[128]</a></sup> The Gupta also had trading links with the Sassanid and Byzantine Empire.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2019)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>. The four-fold varna system was observed under the Gupta period but caste system was fluid. Brahmins followed non-Brahmanical profession as well. Khastriyas were involved in trade and commerce. The society largely coexisted among themselves.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[129]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="Quotation needed from source to verify. (February 2021)">need quotation to verify</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy"><span id="Legacy_of_the_Gupta_Empire">Legacy</span></span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Scholars of this period include <a href="/wiki/Var%C4%81hamihira" title="Varāhamihira">Varāhamihira</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>, who is believed to be the first to consider <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zero" title="Zero">zero</a> as a separate number, postulated the theory that the Earth rotates about its own axis, and studied <a href="/wiki/Solar_eclipse" title="Solar eclipse">solar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lunar_eclipse" title="Lunar eclipse">lunar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eclipses" title="Eclipses">eclipses</a>. <a href="/wiki/Kalidasa" title="Kalidasa">Kalidasa</a>, who was a great playwright, who wrote plays such as <a href="/wiki/Shakuntala" title="Shakuntala">Shakuntala</a>, and marked the highest point of <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit_literature" title="Sanskrit literature">Sanskrit literature</a> is also said to have belonged to this period. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sushruta" title="Sushruta">Sushruta Samhita</a>, which is a Sanskrit redaction text on all of the major concepts of ayurvedic medicine with innovative chapters on surgery, dates to the Gupta period.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Chess" title="Chess">Chess</a> is said to have developed in this period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[130]</a></sup> where its early form in the 6th century was known as <i><a href="/wiki/Chaturanga" title="Chaturanga"><span title="International Alphabet of Sanskrit transliteration"><i lang="sa-Latn">caturaṅga</i></span></a></i>, which translates as "four divisions [of the military]" – <a href="/wiki/Infantry" title="Infantry">infantry</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>, <a href="/wiki/War_elephant" title="War elephant">elephantry</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Chariot" title="Chariot">chariotry</a> – represented by the pieces that would evolve into the modern pawn, knight, bishop, and rook, respectively. Doctors also invented several medical instruments, and even performed operations. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indian_numerals" title="Indian numerals">Indian numerals</a> which were the first <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Positional" title="Positional">positional</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Base_10" title="Base 10">base 10</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Numeral_systems" title="Numeral systems">numeral systems</a> in the world originated from Gupta India. The <a href="/wiki/Hindu_calendar#Weekday/Vāsara" title="Hindu calendar">names of the seven days in a week</a> appeared at the start of the Gupta period based on Hindu deities and planets corresponding to the Roman names. The ancient Gupta text <a href="/wiki/Kama_Sutra" title="Kama Sutra">Kama Sutra</a> by the Indian scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vatsyayana" title="Vatsyayana">Vatsyayana</a> is widely considered to be the standard work on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Human_sexual_behaviour" title="Human sexual behaviour">human sexual behaviour</a> in Sanskrit literature.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Aryabhata" title="Aryabhata">Aryabhata</a>, a noted mathematician-astronomer of the Gupta period proposed that the earth is round and rotates about its own axis. He also discovered that the Moon and planets shine by reflected sunlight. Instead of the prevailing cosmogony in which eclipses were caused by pseudo-planetary nodes <a href="/wiki/Rahu" title="Rahu">Rahu</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ketu_(mythology)" title="Ketu (mythology)">Ketu</a>, he explained eclipses in terms of shadows cast by and falling on Earth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[131]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Art_and_architecture">Art and architecture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Gupta_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Art and architecture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Gupta_art" title="Gupta art">Gupta art</a></div>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed" style="font-size:88%; line-height:130%; border-bottom:1px #aaa solid;">
<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 322px"><div style="width: 322px">

</ul>
<p>The Gupta period is generally regarded as a classic peak of North <a href="/wiki/Indian_art" title="Indian art">Indian art</a> for all the major religious groups. Although painting was evidently widespread, the surviving works are almost all religious sculpture. The period saw the emergence of the iconic carved stone deity in Hindu art, as well as the Buddha-figure and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jain" title="Jain">Jain</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Tirthankara" title="Tirthankara">tirthankara</a></i> figures, the latter often on a very large scale. The two great centres of sculpture were <a href="/wiki/Mathura" title="Mathura">Mathura</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gandhara" title="Gandhara">Gandhara</a>, the latter the centre of <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>. Both exported sculpture to other parts of northern India.
</p><p>The most famous remaining monuments in a broadly Gupta style, the caves at <a href="/wiki/Ajanta_Caves" title="Ajanta Caves">Ajanta</a>, <a href="/wiki/Elephanta_Caves" title="Elephanta Caves">Elephanta</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ellora_Caves" title="Ellora Caves">Ellora</a> (respectively Buddhist, Hindu, and mixed including Jain) were in fact produced under later dynasties, but primarily reflect the monumentality and balance of Guptan style. Ajanta contains by far the most significant survivals of painting from this and the surrounding periods, showing a mature form which had probably had a long development, mainly in painting palaces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle1994118–22,_123–26,_129–35_133-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle1994118–22,_123–26,_129–35-133">[132]</a></sup> The Hindu <a href="/wiki/Udayagiri_Caves" title="Udayagiri Caves">Udayagiri Caves</a> actually record connections with the dynasty and its ministers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199492–97_134-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle199492–97-134">[133]</a></sup> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vishnu_Temple,_Deogarh" title="Vishnu Temple, Deogarh">Dashavatara Temple</a> at <a href="/wiki/Deogarh,_Uttar_Pradesh" title="Deogarh, Uttar Pradesh">Deogarh</a> is a major temple, one of the earliest to survive, with important sculpture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle1994113–14_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJ.C._Harle1994113–14-135">[134]</a></sup>
</p>

<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg"><img alt="Maharaja Sri Gupta inscription on the Allahabad pillar Samudragupta inscription.jpg" data-file-height="256" data-file-width="869" decoding="async" height="29" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg/100px-Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg/150px-Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg/200px-Maharaja_Sri_Gupta_inscription_on_the_Allahabad_pillar_Samudragupta_inscription.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>

<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_I" title="Chandragupta I">Chandra-Gupta I</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg"><img alt="Queen Kumaradevi and King Chandragupta I on a coin.jpg" data-file-height="1752" data-file-width="1715" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg/100px-Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg/150px-Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ec/Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg/200px-Queen_Kumaradevi_and_King_Chandragupta_I_on_a_coin.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>319–335 CE</td>
<td>His title <i>Maharajadhiraja</i> ("king of great kings") suggests that he was the first emperor of the dynasty. It is not certain how he turned his small ancestral kingdom into an empire, although a widely accepted theory among modern historians is that his marriage to the <a href="/wiki/Licchavi_(tribe)" title="Licchavi (tribe)">Licchavi</a> princess Kumaradevi helped him extend his political power.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudra-Gupta</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SamudraguptaCoin.jpg"><img alt="SamudraguptaCoin.jpg" data-file-height="1602" data-file-width="1635" decoding="async" height="98" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/SamudraguptaCoin.jpg/100px-SamudraguptaCoin.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/SamudraguptaCoin.jpg/150px-SamudraguptaCoin.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/SamudraguptaCoin.jpg/200px-SamudraguptaCoin.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>335–375 CE</td>
<td>Defeated several kings of northern India, and annexed their territories to his empire. He also marched along the south-eastern coast of India, advancing as far as the <a href="/wiki/Pallava_dynasty" title="Pallava dynasty">Pallava</a> kingdom. In addition, he subjugated several frontier kingdoms and tribal oligarchies. His empire extended from <a href="/wiki/Ravi_River" title="Ravi River">Ravi River</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Brahmaputra_River" title="Brahmaputra River">Brahmaputra River</a> in the east, and from the Himalayan foothills in the north to central India in the south-west; several rulers along the south-eastern coast were his tributaries.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Kacha_(king)" title="Kacha (king)">Kacha</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg"><img alt="Kachagupta of the Gupta Empire circa AD 335.jpg" data-file-height="387" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="48" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg/100px-Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg/150px-Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/06/Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg/200px-Kachagupta_of_the_Gupta_Empire_circa_AD_335.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>mid 4th century CE</td>
<td>Rival brother/king, possibly an usurper, there are coins who attest him as ruler; possibly identical with <a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudra-Gupta</a>.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Ramagupta" title="Ramagupta">Rama-Gupta</a></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Chandragupta_II" title="Chandragupta II">Chandra-Gupta II <b>Vikramaditya</b></a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg"><img alt="ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg" data-file-height="948" data-file-width="952" decoding="async" height="100" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/100px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/150px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg/200px-ChandraguptaIIOnHorse.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>375–415 CE</td>
<td>Continued the expansionist policy of his father <a href="/wiki/Samudragupta" title="Samudragupta">Samudragupta</a>: historical evidence suggests that he defeated the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Kshatrapas" title="Western Kshatrapas">Western Kshatrapas</a>, and extended the Gupta empire from the <a href="/wiki/Indus_River" title="Indus River">Indus River</a> in the west to the <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region in the east, and from the Himalayan foothills in the north to the <a href="/wiki/Narmada_River" title="Narmada River">Narmada River</a> in the south.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kumaragupta" title="Kumaragupta">Kumara-Gupta I</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg"><img alt="KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg" data-file-height="948" data-file-width="926" decoding="async" height="102" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg/100px-KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg/150px-KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg/200px-KumaraguptaFightingLion.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>415–455 CE</td>
<td>He seems to have maintained control of his inherited territory, which extended from <a href="/wiki/Gujarat" title="Gujarat">Gujarat</a> in the west to <a href="/wiki/Bengal" title="Bengal">Bengal</a> region in the east.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Skandagupta" title="Skandagupta">Skanda-Gupta</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg"><img alt="Skandagupta Circa 455-480 CE.jpg" data-file-height="500" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg/100px-Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg/150px-Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg/200px-Skandagupta_Circa_455-480_CE.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>455–467 CE</td>
<td>It is stated that he restored the fallen fortunes of the Gupta family, which has led to suggestions that during his predecessor's last years, the Empire may have suffered reverses, possibly against the <a href="/wiki/Pushyamitras" title="Pushyamitras">Pushyamitras</a> or the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunas" title="Hunas">Hunas</a>. He is generally considered the last of the great Gupta Emperors.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Purugupta" title="Purugupta">Puru-Gupta</a></td>
<td></td>
<td>467–473 CE</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Kumaragupta_II" title="Kumaragupta II">Kumara-Gupta II <b>Kramaditya</b></a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg"><img alt="Kumaragupta II Kramaditya Circa 530-540 CE.jpg" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="50" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg/100px-Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg/150px-Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg/200px-Kumaragupta_II_Kramaditya_Circa_530-540_CE.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>473–476 CE</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddhagupta" title="Buddhagupta">Buddha-Gupta</a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg"><img alt="Budhagupta in Malwa Circa 476-495 CE.jpg" data-file-height="398" data-file-width="720" decoding="async" height="55" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg/100px-Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg/150px-Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg/200px-Budhagupta_in_Malwa_Circa_476-495_CE.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>476–495 CE</td>
<td>He had close ties with the rulers of <a href="/wiki/Kannauj" title="Kannauj">Kannauj</a> and together they sought to run the <a href="/wiki/Alchon_Huns" title="Alchon Huns">Alchon Huns</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hunas" title="Hunas">Hunas</a>) out of the fertile plains of Northern India.
</td></tr>
<tr>
<td><a href="/wiki/Narasimhagupta" title="Narasimhagupta">Narasimha-Gupta <b>Baladitya</b></a></td>
<td><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg"><img alt="Narasinhagupta I Circa 414-455 AD.jpg" data-file-height="532" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="53" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg/100px-Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg/150px-Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg/200px-Narasinhagupta_I_Circa_414-455_AD.jpg 2x" width="100"/></a></td>
<td>495–530 CE</td>
<td>
</td></tr>
<tr>
